0312600844|1.0|Having read and somewhat liked Franzen's The Corrections in 2001, I was willing to spend time on Freedom and found myself to be disappointed.   I question whether I was reading the same book that received consistently glowing reviews from many of my favorite sources (i.e. New York Times, Salon, Time).  Freedom is merely a mainstream book costumed as a literary novel (some critics called it \\"The Great American Novel\\").    Among other problems, the author struggles with the female voice of Patty Berglund.  Patty's opening confessional may have been Franzen's attempt to disguise his inability to present an authentic female voice.   As other Amazon reviewers have noted, there was not an apparent change of voice once her autobiographical section ended.    The portraits of most of the book's characters and relationships were unconvincing. Walter's development was not believable given his passive nature at the beginning of the novel.  I did not buy the fact that at the end of the novel Walter decides to ponder whether or not to permit Patty back into his life while she shivers for a time in the cold on his porch.  We never really know why he went from a passive, somewhat caring person to this misogynistic character by novel's end.  I wasn't sure if the heavy-handed way that Franzen conveyed Joey's incident with his wedding ring was intended to be funny or satirical.  It was not amusing and a weak attempt at symbolism.  The overabundance of pop culture references were unnecessary unless they are included to help us all believe that the author is hip and with it.     Finally, some critics have referred to Freedom as Franzen's War and Peace.  I would wager that, unlike Tolstoy's novel which we are reading 150 years later, we will not be reading Franzen's novel 150 years from now.  I'll take Updike and Roth any day.

B001ILHY2C|4.0|DEATH RACE delivers pretty much exactly what it sets out to deliver.  If you're expecting anything like convincing character development (or ANY character development), a credible plot or incisive dialogue...you have not read the description of the film or seen any of the previews.  If, however, you are looking for a film with lots of car chase action, gory deaths and more car chase action with a touch of gunfire...then DEATH RACE fits that bill.    It's just a couple of years in the future, but apparently, our economy has gotten worse, and our prison population has exploded.  Run by private companies, the penal system has developed a novel way to make money.  They have inmates chase each other around a lengthy track in the middle of the prison island in souped-up, heavily modified and armored vehicles.  A race to the death.  Millions upon millions of Americans pay up to $99 to get live streams of these races.  (I did the math...apparently each race must gross somewhere between $4.5 and $7 BILLION!)    Jason Statham is framed for the murder of his wife, just so he can be imprisoned and compelled to race.  If he can win, he will be freed and can see his infant daughter again.  You see, Statham was a famous race car driver at one time, but fell on hard times.  Now he, and his rag-tag team of mechanics (led by Ian McShane), must wage battle against the most ferociously resilient moving vehicles ever devised.  And Statham also wants to find out who killed his wife and how the warden (Joan Allen...I hope she received a HUGE paycheck for lowering herself to this junk) is involved.    I will give the movie credit for not skimping on the chase scenes.  They are lengthy, noisy, fast, crazily edited, violent and satisfying on a visceral level.  Many lower budget action films will promise lots of action, but have lots of scenes of endless talking instead.  DEATH RACE gives us a quick, brutal opening race and then three more lengthy races to enjoy during the duration of the film.  The action in the races defies the laws of physics, of course...but there are inventive moments of mayhem.    Jason Statham, who can be fun (THE BANK JOB showed he actually has skills.  The CRANK films show he has a sense of humor) is fairly flat in this film.  Allen plays her part exactly the same throughout.  McShane chews the scenery, but he comes closest to making something of the sketchy dialogue and character he is given.  Tyrese Gibson, as a rival driver delivers yet another flat, uninteresting performance.      I'm actually thinking this film should be 3.5 stars.  It is, on any critical or analytical level, pure junk.  But with your sound system turned to 11 and your brain turned to about 2...it features well-made violence and chaos which can be enjoyed for 90 guilty-pleasure minutes.    (By the way, I haven't seen Roger Corman's DEATH RACE 2000 in decades...but I think the two films bear little resemblance.  Corman gets a producing credit here...but honestly, the film has more in common with THE RUNNING MAN than Corman's cheesy classic.)

It feels as if the album itself is a continuum of the great Avalon, but with the time taken into account. Most of the pieces are there. Ferry took into account his age, and soften his approach, which is nice since it illustrates the maturity clearly without the need to hide it. If he had tried to make it look more like the old Roxy records, a highly possible failure would have disappointed many fans. With Olympia that is not the case. 1416583432|3.0|First of all, most dog lovers will enjoy this book because of how it brings the human into the world of the dog and makes us reconsider all the details of the world from his/her point of view. That said, there are numerous basic scientific flaws in the book, which, while they aren't central to the material, are glaring enough to make me wonder what other flaws the book may contain that i didn't catch. Examples: stating that insects are arachnids (p.20) as well as using \\"family\\" and \\"class\\" interchangeably; and stating that an increase of 10 decibels is a hundredfold rise in sound strength (it is tenfold, as 10 decibels = 1 bel, and the bel is a base-10 logarithmic scale). Maybe she just needed a better editor. I hope her future endeavors will be more carefully inspected before they are published, because such errors make me take all of the scientific reporting in the book with a grain of salt. Other than that, i was left wishing for more specifics about the how and what of dogs' body language, expressions, and movements. The author frequently stated that these things have nuanced meanings, which she gleaned from her hours of painstaking review of her video footage; i only wish she had shared more details of her hard-earned findings. I would love to know better how to communicate with my beloved canine. I was glad that the author did take the opportunity to dispel several common myths about dog training and behavior. I did enjoy the author's often endearing language, such as when she describes that dogs \\"see [humans] as fine general-purpose tools.\\" Overall, the book is a wonderful blend of perspectives on genealogy, history, and sociology of what it means to be a dog. The book succeeded in amplifying my already high regard for my dog -- and in encouraging me to think of myself as a human in a dog's world rather than the other way around.

B001IBIHQ4|5.0|After reading the negative and positive reviews of the The Pacific and finally viewing the DVD set, I have to say that the series is just astounding.  First of all, comparisons to Band of Brothers (BoB) is inevitable but unnecessary given that the battles in the European and the Pacific theatres were fundamentally different as any professional and avid historian will tell you.  The documentary included in the DVD states this quite poignantly.    As a result, the format and the presentation of the Pacific departs from BoB in that it focuses on 3 Marines (and not an entire combat unit as in BoB), and is much darker and cynical given that the Japanese and the Pacific War in general were far more brutal and savage to the American soldier. If you can understand these differences, the Pacific truly shines as it powerfully conveys how the 3 Marines change from gung-ho idealism to men emotionally and physically tempered by unrestrained combat, a relentless enemy, a brutal environment, and survivor's guilt.     And who are these Marines ? John Basilone, a true-believer of the Marine Corp, who wins the Medal of Honor in Guadacanal, but perishes in Iwo Jima.  Throughout the story, you get the sense that he is on borrowed time, but nevertheless lives his life to the fullest knowing well and good that death awaits him.  Bob Leckie, a Marine who enters the Pacific war not completely understanding what he has gotten into, and as a result finds himself on the brink of insanity as he struggles to make sense of the violence around him. And Eugene Sledge, a Marine who at moments of vicious fighting in Okinawa, finds and loses his humanity, and is left to question why he enlisted, the necessity of the war itself, and why he survived when others did not. In this vein, the Pacific is vastly more psychological than physical which is truly where the conflict lies in each man. Not to say that the violent battles depicted in the series are cheap and gratuitous. Quite the contrary.  They arebrutally realistic and assault the very core of these Marines, who are left to comprehend and survive a vicious world turned upside down.    For the reasons cited above, the Pacific is worth owning as it serves to better understand the WWII experience. You won't be disappointed !!

B00006IRJU|4.0|It's easy to see why 'Man Of Colours' became Icehouse's biggest album, and a worldwide hit; it's easily the most consistent album since their near-flawless debut. Although they had some brilliant individual songs and singles, some of Icehouse's other albums didn't quite match the peak of their debut. But there's no doubt about this one; Iva Davies and Co got their mojo back in the best possible way, delivering ten really strong and memorable pop songs.     'Crazy', 'Electric Blue', and `My Obsession' are obvious singles, and deserved their success, but there are plenty of other pop gems here too, such as 'Girl In The Moon' and especially 'The Kingdom' (a personal favourite). The title track is a subdued, haunting number, with a passionate vocal performance from Iva Davies, while 'Sunrise' is an anthemic closer. There are also a couple of surprisingly edgy and hard-driving songs like 'Anybody's War' and the horn-driven 'Nothing Too Serious' (another good vocal performance here).     There is not much that's particularly \\"arty\\" or \\"experimental\\" about this album. For the most part, the music is fairly straightforward mid 80's pop/rock. But 'Man of Colours' stands out because Icehouse were just so good at it. Iva Davies was a gifted composer and musician, and he surrounded himself with people like Rob Kretschmer and Andy Quanta, who were talented instrumentalists in their own right. The result is one of the most consistently strong synthpop albums of its time, full of pop hooks and clever songwriting. 'Man of Colours' should take pride of place in any collection of great 80's pop/rock.      Four stars.
B004132HZS|4.0|I REALY wanted to hate this production, the same way I hate Shakespeare in modern dress, but once viewed on PBS I have to come clean and admit I enjoyed it. Sherlock is played as a hyperactive iconoclast which certainly isn't the way Conan Doyle wrote the character, but it does follow as a sort of pastiche of Jeremy Brett's decidedly off center version.  The modern stories are good but not great, with the first (\\"A Study in Pink\\") my favorite of the three.  Sherlockians will find many an homage to the Doyle stories peppered throughout these three with brother Mycroft, the dolt Lestrade and evil doer Moriarty playing minor parts and certainly laying the ground work to visit a post modern Riechenbach Falls.  Production values are up to typical BBC standards and even in the modern London there is an atmospheric quality that fits the Holmesian canon. In the end I suspect the degree of enthusiasm for this production will hang on generational demographics and good writing.  For me, they are an entertaining respite from the \\"real thing\\", but not something I would view over and over again the way I watch videos of Brett and Rathbone.  Remakes of the Sherlock Holmes stories come and go, both as pastiche and true to text presentations.  Given time this effort will seek it's own level in that growing group.    In my opinion nobody touches Basil Rathbone as the definitive Holmes and no video production can match the Granada series' \\"time period correctness\\" starring Jeremy Brett.  But (and I know this is a video review) in my opinion the very best way to enjoy Sherlock is with a treasured copy of William S. Baring-Gould's \\"The Annotated Sherlock Holmes\\" in your lap and Charles Viney's \\"Sherlock Holmes in London\\" on your end table.  Call me old fashioned, but the best fictional characters come alive in your mind when reading, not watching a DVD.  In this effort I just miss the gas lamps, horse drawn carriages and eventful train trips to lonely estates, so 5 stars for a well done modern take, production values and generally good writing and a star off for the certainty that these will not be in my library of \\"must view over and over\\" DVD's.  BTW:  Has anyone else noticed a more than passing resemblance of this Holmes and Watson to that of Doctors House and Wilson?  Even the surname initials fit....

B000002USN|4.0|I'm not sure that it's established fact that ALL of the duets included on this famous collection were done (shall-we-say?) in that \\"unforgettable\\" Natalie Cole style--with guest artists singing to a pre-recorded vocal (by Ol' Blue eyes, in this case), but it's actually pretty much what I would expect and nothing I'm about to get all het up about.  As controversial as Cole's duet album with her long deceased dad was in some circles, it actually brought new light on what has longsince been common music industry practice--namely, the fact that not all contributors to a project are in the studio at the same time.  Whatever complaints you may have about a potential lack of spontaneity or warmth, it's not uncommon even for duetting singers to never share studio time.      I remember once posting a note on the website of American roots singer Tracy Nelson's website (the singer, not the actress) inquiring about her experiences working with another singer (Eliza Gilkyson) I was interested in on a project album.  Ms Nelson recalled doing the track but acknowledged she had never worked directly w/ Ms Gilkyson, nor had she even met her.  Clearly, it's not just on slick pop projects that not-so-live duets, trios or you-name-the-number is routine practice.  And even if you don't find it ideal, artistically, you've got to admit that it's an interesting phenomenon.  I mean, just how is it done?  Did Sinatra record his half of the song, leaving gaps for the guest artist to fill in, OR did he do the whole thing as a run through, with certain spots being just a \\"working vocal\\" for the second singer to step into?    There are actually some advantages to singing along with a pre-recorded Frank.  For one thing, you get a few chances to work out just how you're going to respond to some of his famous \\"nutty, cuckoo\\" improvisations (with both lyrics and melody).  That seems to be the case on several tracks, particularly the opener 7(\\"The Lady Is A Tramp\\")with Luther Vandross and \\"You Make Me Feel So Young\\" with Charles Aznavour.  Perhaps as a nod to real or imagined feminist critcs, Sinatra mostly changes \\"tramp\\" to \\"CHAMP\\" in the former song, but not always.  In a live duet, it'd be hard for his vocal partner to keep up with Sinatra's constant playing with that (and just about every other) lyric.  Responding to a recording allows Vandross to sing the same text and to play off some of the Chairman's imporvisations and just stay on top of things overall.      As for Aznavour (as it is elsewhere for Julio Iglesias, to some extent), imagine what a relief it is for a non-native speaker of English to NOT have to keep up with Sinatra's slangy and highly idiomatic textual improvisations.  Iglesias has it somewhat easier on \\"Summer Wind,\\" which the usually breezy Sinatra sings fairly straight.  It is, in fact, Iglesias who improvises here and there--playing of his Spanish roots at one point by singing \\"two AMIGOS and the summer wind.\\"  Some may find that endearing; others may think it a bit cutesy.      If Sinatra's vocals weren't \\"live\\" for the tracks with Streisand and Anita Baker, you'd never know it from their warm interplay and conversational insertions.  Tony Bennett and Sinatra are so simpatico that the actual circumstances of their recording of \\"New York, New York\\" scarcely matter.  And Aretha Franklin's vocal on \\"What Now My Love\\" has such grandeur that it's hard to care whether she and Frank shared studio space and went out for coffee after the session.  And speaking of vocal chops, Sinatra still had 'em for this session.  I've heard some clips of him toward the end of his life that weren't nearly as strong, but on DUETS,  he seems to be in rather good voice.    Perhaps I can be such a fan of this record because (ironically) I am not a huge, huge Sinatra fan in general.  It seems that many of the negative reviews stem from ardent admirers of his '40s (and particularly) his '50s work.  If not exactly ardent,  I'm nonetheless an admirer of those recordings, and I find it really quite interesting to hear the older, wiser, if somewhat more vocally frayed singer's re-takes on some of this classic material.  Perhaps if I were more emotionally invested in the older versions,  I would be as indignant as others seem to be that their memory has been tampered with.  I find I can enjoy this CD pretty much for what it is--and isn't.  The comparison/contrasts to earlier work is fascinating.    Is DUETS (and its follow-up) gimmicky product masquerading as art? Was Sinatra just coasting and cashing in at the end of his career?   Well, that's up for individual listeners to judge.  To paraphrase the Rolling Stones, \\"It's only contemporary adult popular music and I like it.\\"

This last figure is sure to change with the introduction of this new version - the same story but made in to the graphic novel format by Johann Sfar, an illustrator par excellence.<br /><br />There will doubtless be purists who prefer the original (very exquisite) illustrations for the book and feel that the presentation of the story in graphic novel format (read `comic book format') diminishes the concept of Saint-Exup&eacute;ry. It is a fable so fine that it has been transformed into a film by Stanley Donen, stage play by several writers including Rick Cummins and John Scoullar, an opera by Rachel Portman, and now another art form - the graphic novel.  For this reader it is successful on every level, and if anything this new edition will introduce this charming fable to millions more readers, young in years or in spirit. Grady Harp, November 10

193413712X|5.0|Is this the most riveting book ever written? No.<br /><br />But then, the Pulitzer Committee is not searching for a roller-coaster thriller likely to be forgotten when finished. According to its guidelines, for its fiction award, it is looking:<br /><br />\\"For distinguished fiction by an American author, preferably dealing with American life...\\"<br /><br />Paul Harding's \\"Tinkers\\" is definitely distinguished, commanding the respect of the reader long afterward.<br /><br />The novel is also distinctly American--set in New England, it echoes the great works of the American Romantics, building upon them in a new and powerful way. Hawthorne's \\"Scarlet Letter\\" is referred to explicitly with great panache. I also felt echoes of Hawthorne's classic short story, \\"Roger Malvin's Burial,\\" a tale about unkept promises and familial loss upon the early American frontier (but perhaps that was my own reader-response).<br /><br />Harding's book retraces the New England landscape of this same American frontier--some centuries later--obliquely. As various tropes surfaced throughout the work, such as hieroglyphics, I was reminded of Emerson's \\"Nature,\\" which also made use of the trope (it was common in a lot of literature of the Romantics--especially after the news of the discovery of the Rosetta Stone). Just as Hawthorne, Emerson, Thoreau, and other American Romantics would explore the deep, ineffable mysteries of Nature, so does Harding, with poetic symbolism, in this modern, transcendentalist masterpiece.<br /><br />That said, don't get too worked up if the novel doesn't make perfect sense on the first read. It is not written chronologically. Instead, Harding writes in Emersonian circles, so to speak; the narrative shifts about, eventually receding deeper and deeper into the past--into the pain, joy, and wonder of being human--even as life is ebbing away. This novel works its magic on the reader--the audience feels a true connection. That is, we leave the world of fiction, walking away down the paths of our own lives, contemplating what is most precious as this ephemeral dream speeds by.away down the paths of our own lives, contemplating what is most precious as this ephemeral dream speeds by.

1594484775|4.0|This is the first Nick Hornby novel I've read, and I was surprised by how thought-provoking and moving it was. Hornby has a reputation as a writer of easygoing, easy-to-read books, and \\"Juliet, Naked\\" is certainly an entertaining read. But it's also funny, smart about the role music can play in fans' lives, and compassionate toward characters uneasily navigating the challenges of middle age.  The relationship between Duncan and Annie is sharply drawn. They're just old enough to start recognizing that what they're living IS their life -- they're not still get ready to live it -- and to feel a bit dissatisfied and uncomfortable with the compromises they've made. For years Annie has tolerated Duncan's fixation on rock musician Tucker Crowe, who mysteriously dropped out of public view after the great success of his album \\"Juliet.\\" Duncan has channeled all his emotional energy into the website about Tucker that he has created, and spends more time communicating with fans across the globe than he does with Annie. For her part, Annie has not expressed her disappointment and has stayed with Duncan because it's the path of least resistance. Hornby does a masterful job of leavening the essential sadness of their relationship with humor, especially Annie's own thoughts about her relationship with Duncan and her reasons for staying in the fading seaside town where they live. Duncan's obsession with Tucker, and his conviction that he can assess people's intelligence and perhaps even their essential worth on the basis of their reaction to his music, is something any serious music fan will recognize (and shiver at). One of the questions \\"Juliet, Naked\\" explores is the line between appreciation and obsession and to what extent being a fan can become a substitute for living one's own life.  This could all have gotten ponderous in other hands, but Hornby keeps the story moving along smartly. When Duncan posts an ecstatic review of the stripped-down \\"Juliet\\" album (the \\"Juliet, Naked\\"of the title) on his webiste, Annie weighs in with her own review, strongly preferring the original version of the album. That these two characters end up learning more about each other's deepest thoughts through online postings than they have through face-to-face conversations is entirely believable. What happens next is a stretch, but a stretch in the best novelistic tradition, as relationships get shaken up and characters' inner lives rearranged.  \\"Juliet, Naked\\" is, among other things,  a loving but sharp-eyed exploration of what it means to be a music fan (or to love art in any form). It never feels as if Hornby is putting his characters down -- even Duncan is sympathetic. Rather, he seems to be encouraging readers to recognize that time passes by, all the time, and to focus on what's important while time is still left. That he manages this without getting boring or preachy is a real achievement.

It makes us want to go buy books on English History to read up on what really happened at that time.  It makes us want to read Eliot's \\"Murder in the Cathedral\\" to get a sense of what happened to create such a schism between Church and Palace.  And so on ...<br /><br />It is writing like that that makes historical fiction thrilling - there is a palette that already exists, and the author skilfully blends the story of seemingly ordinary people into it. Follett also coupled with \\"Pillars\\" a grand story of cathedral building, which awed even someone who is normally bored by discussions that are more at home in books on structural engineering.  \\"Pillars\\" was on par with \\"I, Claudius\\" and its more kitschy (but spellbinding nonetheless) cousin \\"The First Man in Rome.\\"<br /><br />\\"World Without End\\" does not hurdle the bar that \\"Pillars\\" set.  There's lots of building and structural engineering, but very little of the part that makes Historical novels interesting to this reader.  There is a tease at the start of the novel, to create a link with the great succession battle of the day, replete with hints of royal intrigue.  But at the end of day, there isn't very much that's interesting.  The Plague does make it in to the story, but in a very non-gripping fashion, as do some campaigns in France.<br /><br />That said, the core story is good.  It is well-written, with interesting characters - especially its leading female protagonist, who would have been considered \\"forward\\" in the 1960s, let alone in 14th century England.  The length of the book did not bother me - in fact, I was happy for it, since it gave me something to look forward to doing for several evenings after putting family to bed.  And it did inspire me to go to Wikipedia to read up on some of the Kings of that time.  But all in all, I was disappointed, compared to the thrill that \\"Pillars\\" gave me.

031603438X|5.0|What a fun biography! What a life!<br /><br />Keith Richards is definitely my favorite heroin addict, ever.<br /><br />Random observations:<br /><br />--He refreshingly avoids recovery-speak in discussing his legendary drug abuse.  Consequently this may be one of the best firsthand accounts of it ever written - clear, plain, detailed. I'd rather read this than Aldous Huxley or The Beats. While not encouraging anyone else to try it, he doesn't apologize or lather on phony regrets . He enjoyed it while he did it.  A lot of it was just business for touring musicians - something to get you up for the next show on your grueling schedule,  and something to mellow off the first drug's hard edges after the show. He figures he stayed alive because he used pure products (often obtained, albeit illegally, from prescriptions in England, where it was legal), and was meticulous about not overdoing it. There's a jolly scene where he describes himself cutting Turkish heroin exactly 97 to 3. Not 96 to 4.<br /><br />-- He's down to earth. More genuine in some ways than Jagger, whom he faults for accepting a knighthood after playing the rebel his entire life. (A class thing perhaps: Jagger the middle-class, good-student striver ultimately wanting acceptance by the elites; Richards the son of a factory worker, knowing that's not his bag and not really wanting it.) He'd rather hang with musicians, particularly good ones, than the jet set and Eurotrash.<br /><br />--He never turns to Buddhism, rants about politics or devotes himself to saving the planet. For this alone I'd lionize him.<br /><br />-- Richards prefers the band to the solo; for him the big moment is when the sound blends and you can't tell who's playing what. He likes hanging with his best buds, most of whom have been in jail. He's comfortable with black people in contexts most whites never reach - Rastafarians in remote villages where most white people would get shot, all-night parties with black musicians on the other side of the tracks after shows in the still-segregated South.<br /><br />--He really has led a charmed life, wriggling out of numerous busts where they had him cold - in Canada, Honolulu, Arkansas, and England. He's also survived auto wrecks and fires, physical mayhem and rioting English teenage girls, whom he regarded as scarier than the cops who staked him out for years trying to catch him with drugs.<br /><br />--Oh my God: all the women. Sigh. It's good to be king.<br /><br />Now for the pontificating. This is one of the most important books in rock history in recent years. Popular culture knows a hell of a lot about the Beatles but far less about the Stones. What folks know about them,  they tend to know about Jagger instead of Richards. And what they know about Richards is disproportionately his indestructibility in the face of unbelievable drug abuse.<br /><br />Which is a pity. Let's not forget that the Rolling Stones were there at the conception, just like the Beatles. Teenyboppers rioted for them, just like for the Beatles.  In 1964, two British polls showed them more popular than the Fab Four. Their rise was seen as heralding the Apocalypse, probably more so than the Beatles. Stones mania in England caught up with the Beatles by 1964 or 1965. The two bands would coordinate their singles' releases so as not to step on each other's hits. By the age of peak cultural and political rebellion, the Beatles were already breaking up while the Stones were just hitting their stride.<br /><br />While Lennon and McCartney were the latest pop-standard immortals, the Stones saw themselves as bluesmen. They singlehandedly brought the legacy of the Chicago blues to an enormous worldwide audience, reviving many blues careers.  Their merging of early rock and roll and Chicago blues created what you today think of as rock - that big pounding sound filling stadiums. No one has ever surpassed them in its execution. Richards refers to them without braggadocio as the world's greatest rock and roll band, and that's true.<br /><br />So much of that can be attributed to Richards, their guitarist for half a century. He was never a glossy pop celebrity.  He had bad teeth. He never came across as a virtuoso a la Clapton or Hendrix. But he and Charlie Watts were - I'm stealing a phrase from the book here - the band's engine house, while Jagger sang and danced out front, the band's public face.<br /><br />Richards was mesmerized during youth by the blues, but unlike a lot of older blues purists, he also loved  rock and roll. The band's early insistence on playing it raised hackles among their base of blues fans; Richards parallels this to folkie disapproval of rock and roll. Richards, Jagger and Brian Jones spent two or three years in poverty singlemindedly pursuing the blues. They dissected every record they could find to replicate its sounds. And they really got it. Early American audiences hearing them on the radio couldn't tell if they were white or black. Richards' life changed when he first heard Elvis singing &#34;Heartbreak Hotel&#34; on a crackling Radio Luxemburg broadcast, but it was Elvis's guitarist Scotty Moore he really idolized.<br /><br />He describes how music is made, how he and Jagger wrote songs, how a sound was achieved, recording tricks. His discovery of five-string tuning - removing a guitar's lowest string and tuning the others like a banjo - changed the Stones' sound.<br /><br />The personal data intrigues, and not just the inside dope on his relationships with Ronnie Spector, Anita Pallenberg, Patti Hansen, Uschi Obermaier and others. Readers may be surprised to learn Richards was a devoted Boy Scout patrol leader and thinks it shaped him into someone who could run a band. Or that he was in a prize-winning boys choir. Or that he was nervous approaching women. Or that in later life he's become a devoted reader, preferring history (World War II, the Romans) Patrick O'Brian's &#34;Master and Commander&#34; series, and George MacDonald Fraser's &#34;Flashman&#34; books. (I salute his excellent taste.)<br /><br />The way to view his life is this: it's not a recommendation to everyone else to screw countless women, including gorgeous models, beautiful revolutionaries, black strippers, groupies and bankers' wives. It's not a recommendation to lead a jangled lifestyle for decades abusing every drug available while putting in recording studio sessions measured in days, not hours, without sleep.<br /><br />Richards is, more or less, a god in the Greek sense, and we marvel at him because he does things that most of us can't or don't really want to. He's unkillable. He's mega-talented, fabulously rich and famous. He has lived a charmed existence by his own rules. But this life killed or destroyed many around him weaker, less lucky or talented than he. Brian Jones was gone by 1969. Richards is the exception that proves these rules. That's the role of gods and kings.<br /><br />Don't try this at home. But it's sure fun to read about.lf a century. He was never a glossy pop celebrity.  He had bad teeth. He never came across as a virtuoso a la Clapton or Hendrix. But he and Charlie Watts were - I'm stealing a phrase from the book here - the band's engine house, while Jagger sang and danced out front, the band's public face.  Richards was mesmerized during youth by the blues, but unlike a lot of older blues purists, he also loved  rock and roll. The band's early insistence on playing it raised hackles among their base of blues fans; Richards parallels this to folkie disapproval of rock and roll. Richards, Jagger and Brian Jones spent two or three years in poverty singlemindedly pursuing the blues. They dissected every record they could find to replicate its sounds. And they really got it. Early American audiences hearing them on the radio couldn't tell if they were white or black. Richards' life changed when he first heard Elvis singing &#34;Heartbreak Hotel&#34; on a crackling Radio Luxemburg broadcast, but it was Elvis's guitarist Scotty Moore he really idolized.  He describes how music is made, how he and Jagger wrote songs, how a sound was achieved, recording tricks. His discovery of five-string tuning - removing a guitar's lowest string and tuning the others like a banjo - changed the Stones' sound.  The personal data intrigues, and not just the inside dope on his relationships with Ronnie Spector, Anita Pallenberg, Patti Hansen, Uschi Obermaier and others. 0684830485|4.0|Set during the Spanish American War of the 1930s, For Whom the Bell Tolls is a novel about an American sent on a mission to help guerrilla fighters blow up a bridge.<br /><br />There. That's the basic plot. It sounds like something out a an action movie, doesn't it. To stop there, however, would be doing the novel and the author a grave disservice.<br /><br />When it gets down to it, For Whom the Bell Tolls is about death and how to face it. The men on this secret bridge-exploding mission are going to die, and they know it. They will not be coming back from this mission. Each of them will be facing death one way or another, and they each have to deal with it individually. The idea of suicide is even approached from a soldier's point of view; the question is raised, is it preferable to kill oneself than to be captured by the enemy?<br /><br />For Whom the Bell Tolls also touches upon racism and politics, though not strictly from an American point of view.<br /><br />Within this tale, violence is to be found in spades, but this is not a simple action story. This is true literature, told in the best writing Hemingway ever did (yes, I admit that's arguable, but it's my opinion ... believe what you want).<br /><br />As a writer, is there anything not to learn from Hemingway? The man knew how to write tense scenes and deal with weighty subject matters all while using as few words as possible, and most of those short words. No other author in my experience has quite been able to do that.<br /><br />As an aside, I'll admit that every time I read a Hemingway novel, I always hate it. Until I reach the very last page. Then suddenly I love it. Not sure why that is, but it's always been my reaction.

David Tennant does his best with what he's given to work with and is fantastic as always (he will be sorely missed, and I eagerly await the next multi-Doctor reunion special), but this collection clearly shows that head writer Russel T. Davies had worn out his welcome by the end. Avoid wasting money on this set, unless you just have to have a complete collection of NuWho.

B003EV6DBM|4.0|Let's face it, we were all skeptical of Matt Smith. How could a young nobody be the Doctor after David Tennant had defined the role for a generation? Luckily for us, and for the franchise, Matt Smith proved to be absolutely incredible in his role, and has re-defined the Doctor! Smith has a decided alien-ness to him, completely different to Tennant's more anguished and angsty humanity. In his mannerisms, he brings about an innate sense of being older than his appearance would suggest, sometimes as crotchety as Hartnell and sometimes as paternal as Pertwee, while still only being a few years older than fan unfavorite Doctor Colin Baker was when he took the role. Smith also models himself on Tom Baker, regarded by many to be the best Doctor ever, and Patrick Troughton, and his synthesis is simply sublime. Smith is, like all great Doctors, a delight to watch.    Now let's get to the writing and episodes. Russel T. Davies has left, and the new showrunner is Steven Moffat, mastermind behind some of the greatest episodes of the NuWho. This is a new, more refined version of the show in many ways. The production values feel higher, although there are some hilariously bad special effects (but, then again, what is Doctor Who without bad special effects?) here and there. Series composer Murray Gold is more restrained and less melodramatic and bombastic in his scoring, although the soundtrack is still pure Gold--possibly his best score yet--and the writing is much more refined. It's not perfect--for example, the Dalek episode feels rushed and should have been a two-parter, whereas the Silurian episode was plodding and should have been a one-parter--but Steven Moffat is shaping up to be the best head writer since Robert Holmes.    Now, a rundown of the episodes:    1. The Eleventh Hour: Our madcap introduction to Eleven and new companion Amy Pond. Fast, fun, and with tantalizing hints at a season-long story arc.    2. The Best Below: A solid episode filled with fun and frights, even if the ending lacks subtlety. Also contains a great segue into the next episode...    3. Victory of the Daleks: Though this Dalek-centric episode is highly polarizing and the climax and conclusion to the episode feels rushed and sloppy (hence the need for a two-parter), it's still a decent episode, with Winston Churchill!    4/5. The Time of Angels/Flesh and Stone: The return of Moffat's pet monsters, the Weeping Angels, and of River Song at her smuggest. Though the Angels' MO is different from their first appearance, this is a tense and thrilling two-parter that is to Series 3's \\"Blink\\" what \\"Aliens\\" was to \\"Alien.\\"    6. Vampires in Venice: The Doctor does his best to prevent Amy from falling in love with him by taking her and her fiance Rory to Venice. Unfortunately, there are vampire fish-people there. A fun, if mediocre episode.    7. Amy's Choice: The Doctor, Amy, and Rory must determine the difference between fantasy and reality while fighting against a caustic, sardonic enemy agent known as the Dream Lord before time runs out. A tense, fun episode with a great villain.    8/9. The Hungry Earth/Cold Blood: This two-parter re-introduces the Silurians and is made in the vein of a Third Doctor/UNIT episode from the '70s--that is to say, it's twice as long as it needs to be and slow-paced. Though somewhat unremarkable, the last ten minutes contain a shocking twist that's not to be missed.    10. Vincent and the Doctor: The Doctor and Amy visit Vincent van Gogh and fight an invisible monster only he can see. The monster is so poorly-CGI'd that it's almost embarrassing (which is why it should have been kept invisible), and is so ancillary to the plot that it could have been left out entirely. The real draw of this episode is the pathos-driven interactions between the Doctor and van Gogh, culminating in a tearjerking finale.    11. The Lodger: An Amy-light episode that's not as bad as it sounds. Separated from his TARDIS, the Doctor moves in with a mediocre guy(played by a mediocre comedian who, thankfully, is not the comic relief of this episode) in order to investigate a space-time disturbance. A surprising funny episode!    12/13. The Pandorica Opens/The Big Bang: The entire universe is at stake as the grand story arc Moffat has been building up to reaches its climax. This finale is different from anything seen in NuWho, with a kind of restraint that was missing from Russel T. Davies' cheesy finales and enough masterful foreshadowing throughout the season to make every theme, every motif of the season build up to the incredible, mind-blowing finale. This is something unprecedented in the new series, I'll leave it at that!    Overall, Series 5 has its highs and lows, but unlike the past four series, the lows are simply mediocre. No farting aliens, no \\"Love and Monsters,\\" no \\"Last of the Time Lords,\\" no faux-regeneration, no \\"End of Time.\\" Whereas Russel T. Davies' era was marred by episodes so bad it made me almost ashamed to enjoy Doctor Who, Moffat's era is, so far, characterized by episodes so good it makes me feel a childlike sense of wonder and enthusiasm I though I'd long outgrown. Let's hope he and Matt Smith can keep up the good work! All Hail Grand Moff Steven!

159102594X|4.0|Joe Abercrombie's the Blade Itself, the opening book of the First Law trilogy, is a very impressive debut novel. The Blade Itself probably deserves a 4.5 star, rather than a 4 star, rating. Briefly, the story takes place in a medieval setting, as fantasy novels so often do, taking place primarily in a large kingdom (called the Union) and tracks the various characters as the nation moves towards war with the barbarian tribes of the north. All the while, the apparently evil empire in the south has a new emperor who is eager to regain that which the empire lost to the Union in a previous war. In all, it's a fairly standard fantasy set up. Where the novel excels, however, is in the characters and the execution.    The characters were truly my favorite part of the Blade Itself. For the most part, the characters are extremely realistic - none are entirely good, none are entirely evil. Most of them are arguably bad people, or at least have done many terrible things in the past, but at the same time, they are all decidedly human. They have weaknesses, strengths, hopes and fears. Glokta, for example, was a rising star in the Union army, a noble of good blood, a champion fencer, etc., etc. but was captured and tortured during war with the southern empire. He emerged crippled, constantly in pain, and took a position as a government inquisitor, whose occupation typically involves torturing suspected criminals into confessing to crimes they may or may not have committed. He is truly a tortured soul - he's utterly miserable and seems to hate everything, including himself.     Another character that I found particularly appealing is that of Logen Ninefingers. Logen is in some ways the prototypical barbarian fantasy character. A great warrior, brutal, typically ruthless, uncouth, etc. But he's been through so many battles and lost so much that he's become a reluctant warrior. Indeed, despite the fact that he is a tremendous killer, he's probably the closest thing to a decent personin the novel. Abercrombie develops his characters nicely. Even those which initially seem like the proverbial one-trick pony, such as Jezal, show growth.    Another major strength of the Blade Itself is in Abercrombie's writing. Although he's capable of writing some fine descriptive narrative, he typically leaves a lot to the imagination of the reader. I'm not sure I could really describe the physical appearance of most of the characters. Personally, I like that. I don't think we typically need to know every minute detail of a characters appearance. This same minimalism is extended to scenery, buildings, battles, etc. He tells as at least as much as we need to know, sometimes more, but rarely burdens us with excessive detail. One particularly strength of the writing, in my opinion, lies in the fact that Abercrombie tailors each chapter to the character who is narrating that chapter. For example, a Logen chapter will be much gruffer, rife with some odd grammatical choices, some slang, etc. A Glokta chapter will seem much bitterer, rife with sarcastic thoughts. Although many authors write different chapters from different perspectives, few show a demonstrable difference in the writing style dependent on the character. It's rather unique and definitely well done.    Abercrombie also injects a fair bit of sarcastic humor into the novel. It's rarely laugh out loud (though some of the descriptions of the barbarian, Logen, exploring the great modernized city were pretty darn funny). Abercrombie's wit is similar to that of Douglas Adams (though certainly not in the same ball park quality-wise). It's definitely worth a chuckle here and there. Finally, as far as writing is concerned, I think Abercrombie writes much more realistic fight scenes than most authors. Most fantasy fight scenes seem to involve two classy swords man who fight epic duel with grace and dignity.  Abercrombie imagines (and, I think, rightfully so) his fight scenes as brutal fights to the death. Characters cheat, fight dirty, do whatever they can to survive. It's a refreshing change in the fantasy panorama.    If I have any complaint about the Blade Itself is that its plot is really nothing unique or special. As I said above, the plot is pretty much standard fantasy fare. Civilized kingdom deals with a barbarian invasion, with a dark, evil empire looming in the shadows. In that regard, the first novel is somewhat predictable. There aren't any real twists or turns, no major surprises. From what I understand, the subsequent novels become more unpredictable, however. My other major criticism is that, well, not a whole lot really happens in the Blade Itself. It reads as one long, extended introduction. This makes sense, of course, because the trilogy was originally intended as one novel and was broken into three. But the novel is basically all setup, introduction and development. The plot doesn't really advance until the last couple hundred pages.    In sum, the Blade Itself is really good and the series has a ton of potential. If the next two books pick up the pace, and the plot starts to unfold in more unpredictable ways, the First Law trilogy could be truly special. The best thing I can ever say about a book is that I want to read more. Well, I do. I finished the Blade Itself and ordered the next two books in the series within an hour. Do yourself a favor and pick it up. You won't regret it.

B004132HZS|5.0|I have been frustrated for the longest time with regard to adaptations of Sir Arthur Conan Doyle's stories that don't stay true to the general spirit of the books. Typically, Holmes and Watson are cast much too old. Watson is always some bumble-brained goofball who just tags along for the ride, almost like a comic sidekick to ask the \\"stupid\\" questions that most people would ask of a brilliant mind like that possessed by Sherlock Holmes. As if Holmes would have the patience to deal with such an idiot?! And the story lines are nearly always garbled beyond recognition.    But anyone who has read even one of the stories knows that these other adaptations, while they may have some fine acting and story-telling going on, fall far from the flavor of the written works. Holmes is sarcastic, socially-inept (most of the time), methodical, more than a touch OCD, etc. Watson is health-conscious, a good doctor, not an idiot, fishes Holmes out of serious trouble on more than one occasion, and has taken it upon himself to chronicle what he sees as the brilliant career of his friend, Holmes.    Though this contemporary adaptation does not stick to the letter of the books, it is fantastic, and that's coming from someone who gets really irritated when movie adaptations stray too far from the details of a well-written story. My only (mild) source of irritation is that Watson doesn't have his hand gun until the third episode. While the details from the stories are present to some degree, they are sometimes mixed up a bit. But there has obviously been a sincere effort made to stick to the feel of the original stories, which is why I am happy to give a five-star review.

B001IA2PTK|2.0|Indy, Indy, Indy...or better yet, Lucas and Spielberg how did you go wrong? The first thought that comes to mind if you read that first line is that I am going to rip this INDIANA JONES AND THE KINGDOM OF THE CRYSTAL SKULL a new one. I am afraid that I am going to be that guy in the room or that guy on those message boards who seems to disagree with everybody when it comes to the newest installment of the INDIANA JONES saga. I will be as honest as I can and explain where I am coming from without revealing too much. I am basing my evaluation of this movie on the three that have come before it. RAIDERS OF THE LOST ARK, THE TEMPLE OF DOOM and THE LAST CRUSADE are all classics. Some might argue that RAIDERS is the best of the series and I couldn't agree more, but I am noticing that with every new installment, something is missing. The story is not as strong, substance is out the door and the wow factor(special effects) is brought in to mesmerize the crowds. I guess this time around, Spielberg and company ran out of substance and wow factor; and realized, in the middle of the shoot, that it is no easy task to duplicate the originality, the wonder or the spectacle of the first movie. They move on ahead anyway and give us this movie that feels more like a nostalgia piece, than a solid entry in the franchise.  The movie starts off with our favorite archeologist, in a tight situation, as usual; but this time around the Russians are the bad guys with Cate Blanchett as their typical leader whose stares are more of an effective weapon than this supernatural ability bestowed upon her by Spielberg. I guess if that talent had actually worked there would be no need for old INDIANA. Dr. JONES played by Harrison Ford is older in this new movie; a relic of the 40's stuck in the new and convoluted world of the 50's stuffed with  everything from Elvis Presley to  Communist paranoia, the nuclear bomb, Area 51, spies, men in black , hot rods and greasers. Indy looked like he had been transported to our time period in a key scene at the beginning of the movie. You won't forget the perilous moment, I assure you. That scene sets the tone and shows that a new dawn in warfare was definitely upon the world. On that note, this is where all of my problems with this movie start. Where are the other perilous moments? Where is the escape by the skin of your teeth moments that this franchise is known for? After the scene that I make reference to, there is no real hold your breath, grab your arm chair moment. INDY 4 feels like it was going through the motions, giving us action scenes with no heart and bigger than life visuals that would have been much more impressive if the climax was not so sluggish and all too familiar.  In this movie, Spielberg and company did not follow my golden rule for making a sequel \\"Go with what works\\". They have been following the road map they have laid since Raiders and it has worked for all three previous movies. It's simple: Action, then talk, more action then more talk, back to the action and finally the climax. Keep the audience stimulated throughout the whole experience. This is the first time in an Indiana Jones movie that the whole thing comes to a halt. Nothing was happening. They start talking about the skulls and then we go to South America, fight some second rate bad guys, find the crystal skull, then Indy's old flame and his  mentor. That was a big whole chunk of the movie with nothing really exciting happening. There were some comedic moments along the way, but the thrills were definitely absent. When the action finally starts, it looks like Deja Vue.How many times are you going to give us the chase/fight scene before you try something different Mr. Spielberg and Lucas? I mean all tree movies have the bad guys chasing Indy in their trucks only to have him jump from his vehicle to beat the crap out of them. They were in the Amazon for goodness sake; Why not a boat fight this time? If you are going to do the same thing atleast try to do better than the last three pictures. Surpass what you've done before or do as good. The chase scene felt like it was put together by a first time director trying to imitate Spielberg's work. The action was happening, but the wit and the thrills, like I said, were somehow non existent. Not until the tenth minute mark before the ending, we get a new twist on the old Indy going toe to toe with the big bad guy who seems to beat the hell out of him first. You won't forget that scene with its voracious extras. I can already hear some fans saying that this movie is as good as Raiders of the lost Ark. What? Did they see the movie I saw? Because it is Spielberg, he gets a free pass? If somebody else was at the helm of this project, moviegoers would have canned this picture.   I really do think Lucas and Spielberg could have given us more and push the limit of their creativity, instead of relying on the love of the character to fill the seats at the movie theater. With so much competition out there, like a National Treasure, for example, which touches on the same subject(action, adventure); Spielberg and Lucas needed to show the younglings who is the best and why they are the best. I predict there will be a debate over this movie for years to come, and it will be between those who just wanted to see Harrison Ford with his famous hat one last time and those who love this series because it is supposed to be an action adventure piece. After the inside jokes and the walk down memory lane, you got to give the younger folks something that is going to keep them stimulated or they will just jump ship for the next big summer thrill ride. After watching Kingdom of the Crystal Skull, I was not raving about what I had seen, but I was drawn to go watch the first three INDIANA JONES because they were fresh and exciting.  Like your first love, you never forget the first of a franchise. Unfortunately, I am sad to report that, INDIANA 4 has fallen under the dreaded sequel curse.

B003Y58CKS|5.0|It is difficult to imagine that the Virgin release of Verdi's Rigoletto will be bettered in the near future. Musically, vocally and dramatically this is one of the most satisfying performances of this opera that I have heard and seen. There is not a whiff of routine or cliche. Maestro Luisi conducts on the brisk side but never at the expense of the music or the singer; indeed those arching Verdian melodies are always given full value. And it must be added that the three leads give more than full value. There isn't an Italian among them but their unerring sense of style and how the music should go compensate for a performance that could have emanated from the stage of La Scala. Among the information that Amazon provides is the \\"timing\\" of the set:90 minutes! This couldn't be more inaccurate. If one checks the timings for most of the sets, the opera runs about 128 minutes. A major exception is the Met set which is alleged to run 180 minutes! I heard and saw this set some years ago, but it is clearly off the mark. I have the ROH set and the timing is listed at 170 minutes. I relistened to it and it checks in at around 128 minutes. There are extras which may boost the timing to 170 but this figure clearly is off the mark as far as the opera is concerned.    As far as Juan diego Florez is concerned, this set is significant. After the run he announced that he was dropping the role! A pity as he is ideal, but the man knows his voice better than we do; thus we must respect his decision and be thankful that it at least has been memorialized. His decision is interesting in that one of his predecessors counted the Duke as one of his signature roles. Tito Schipa has left records documenting his mastery of the role; I assume that he sang it at the old Met--a very large house, larger than any in Europe. Schipa did nothave a large voice: old 78's document this as well as his repertoire. If I am wrong, I trust that corrections will follow. At any rate conductors did respect vocal size in those days. Remember too, that de Luca was considered a great Rigoletto and he was not blessed with an immense voice, a Warren size voice. Florez is marvelous, elegant, musical and charmiing in his depravity. All the duke's arias are rendered with a bel canto sensitivity which surely is what Verdi intended. Damrau matches her Duke with equal musicality and sensitivity. I can only repeat myself by sayiing that her two great arias are exquisitely molded with legato and beauty. As coloraturas go she has a larger voice than, say Galli-Curci, but no less sensitivity to line. Zeljko Lucic (sorry no diacriticals) may not be Italian and the tone may not be as \\"sappy\\" as Warren's (or another greatly underrated American baritone, Robert Weede). But it is a performance to be reckoned with: dramatically and musically satisfyiing, involved and committed. I have to make mention of the Sparafucile: Georg Zeppenfeld. A young man blessed with a black sound that is reminiscent of Gottlob Frick--high praise. Is there a Haen, a Hunding in his future? One can hope.    As to the production I suspect I will have to hedge a bit. Lenhoff is responsible for a somewhat goofy reimagining of Lohengrin, an updating of Dialogue of the Carmelites from Hamburg, a wonderful Tristan from Glyndebourne and a Parsifal that is a mixture of the outre and the wonderful. In the two Wagner opera there are bonus lectures provided which are pedantic in the extreme. One can hardly absorb half of what he is saying. Poulenc and Verdi are spared his academic pontificating. He has updated Poulenc to the (presumably) Nazi era. this has had little or no effect on the opera; after all the henchmen of the French revolution were thugs of the same stripe as the SS. Rigoletto in its time did, of course, have political ramifications and Verdi was obligated to make changes in the source material. As near as I can determine Lenhoff has not offered any political parallel. The Duke's court is dissolute as is the Duke, the courtiers support this amorality: in other words the essentials of Rigoletto are followed in a way that most of us are used to. That the costumes are (more or less) contemporary is really not important and no one wanting a first class Rigoletto should let this be a barrier to acquiring this set.

0374290024|5.0|Ian Morris combines the best aspects of Jared Diamond, Paul Kennedy, David Landes, and Thomas Friedman while contributing his own fascinating insights as an archaeologist in this insightful and fascinating book.  Why The West Rules-For Now is  solid world history, beginning with the first hominid developments and stretching to the early twenty first century CE.  The primary question Morris asks throughout deals with which area, West or East, had the advantage and was more highly developed during each historical period.<br /><br />But Why The West Rules is more than just history.  Morris roams freely throughout the time periods, using economic data and statistical analysis to extrapolate alternative scenarios leading to very different outcomes.  These counterfactuals are always fascinating, none more so than the one with which Morris begins his book, describing an abject Queen Victoria humbly performing the kowtow before a triumphant Chinese overlord.<br /><br />Even more fascinating and important are the final chapters, in which Morris speculates on future developments and posits several possible scenarios for the next hundred years.  These are also solidly grounded and are convincingly argued. They make Why The West Rules-For Now a book one hopes national and cultural leaders will read and discuss  for many years to come.

0439023521|5.0|I know a book is good when I put off all other responsibilities, including sleep, in order to finish it. And even though this is classified as a 'young adult' novel, I enjoyed it perhaps even more than other 'adult' novels I've read recently. It deals with a very mature concept without being too graphic, and at times is very thought provoking.     If you have read Koushun Takami's \\"Battle Royale\\"  then the idea behind \\"The Hunger Games\\" will be familiar. In an effort to keep their citizens in a state of constant fear and passivism, the Capitol of Panem (formerally the USA, renamed after a violent uprising of citizens against the government)has created the \\"Hunger Games\\", an annual contest to the death between 24 kids between the ages of 12 and 18. One boy and one girl are randomly picked from each of the Capitol's outlying 12 districts. When the story begins, it is reaping day, when the 'tributes', as they are called, are chosen.At age 16, Katniss Everdeen has already had to endure four reapings in District 12, and she has had the fortune of never being picked. But this is the first year her younger sister Prim has been required to enter, and wouldn't you know that she is picked and Katniss volunteers to take her place. To make matters worse, the male tribute of District 12 is a boy she owes her very life to--Peeta Mellark, the son of a baker who purposly risked a beating from his mother in order to give Katniss bread when she was on the verge of starvation several years earlier.    Just in the first few chapters Suzanne Collins has set the pace for an exciting, violent and emotional story that never loosens its grip on the reader. Katniss is caught in a whirlwind of activity where she must prepare to kill or be killed, but the complications of having Peeta involved make the situation much more difficult, as there can only be one winner of the Hunger Games.   Katniss is an easy character to like and root for, even when she is being completely oblivious to certain situations right in front of her. Her main goal is survival, and she will do whatever it takes to win. The deaths are handled very tastefully and are never too graphic. The romance is also subdued, no more than kissing, but adds just the right amount of extra suspense and problems for Katniss to deal with.   You can't read this book and not help to wonder if something like this could ever happen in the civilized world. Could one government become so powerful that the could keep an entire population controlled to point that no one thinks twice about children being sent off to kill one another? How long would people allow it to go one before rising up against the government and taking control again? By the end of \\"the Hunger Games\\" it is clear that another revolution is brewing, and Katniss is right in the middle of it. Almost as soon as I finished this book I rushed out to pick up \\"Catching Fire\\". Collins has created a mature read for anyone, though some parents might want to read it for themselves first before deciding if it is appropraite for their kids. If anything, it should encourage some family discussion about the boundaries of power and how one maintains individualism in the face of overwhelming subjection. I can't wait to see how Katniss's story ends.

B000W7JWUA|5.0|Settlers of Catan is one of those games that keeps you coming back for more. The game changes every time you play, so you need to invoke new strategies to win for every game. 3 or 4 people can play, but it's the most fun to play with 5 or 6 (you can buy an extension that allows you to play with more people). After playing only 2 games of Catan, I realized I needed to buy the extemsion because this game is just that fun. And now it's even MORE fun! 20 games later, I'm still fully enjoying it! Players must achieve victory by collecting 10 Victory Points. Victory Points are acquired by building settlements and cities, and by collection Victory Point Cards. Players collect various resources throughout the game which they can use to build roads and settlements etc. The beauty of this game is the fact that a 10 year old can enjoy it just as much as a 50 year old. The 10 year old will build whatever he can to try to achieve victory, the 50 year old will try to base his game play on strategy. And believe me, there is so much strategy involved in Catan. Trading is an important aspect of the game. Players trade resources with each other so they can build more on the board. A fun twist me and my friends add to trading is this: Any deal is fair. Where some people would only trade resources, we trade ANYTHING. This means we could agree to not build on a certain spot on the game board if the person gives us the resource we want. It also means that we could pay someone $1 for one of there resources if we so choose. This special rule really adds a lot to the game because trading is important. Catan is an AMAZING game, I'd recommend it to anyone! It's my favorite board game I've ever played, and I love board games.

Patrick Wilson has a small part as the office hunk and he is charming and likeable without bogging the story or the comedy down with heavy romance.<br /><br />Director Roger Michell who's brought us such gems as Changing Lanes and Notting Hill, has delivered another one. He lets great actors do great acting and he brings us to a world that I found fascinating and that I haven't seen too much of in movies, which is: what it takes to produce a live television broadcast. B002ZG99N6|4.0|** Minor spoilers ahead **<br /><br />A daring bank heist: four armed robbers in heavy disguises hit a bank in urban Boston, executing every detail with clockwork precision - but despite their precautions, one of the employees manages to trigger a silent alarm, and the cops interrupt them.  Taking one of the managers (Rebecca Hall) as hostage, they flee the scene and manage a getaway without being apparently identified, but paranoid about what the bank manager may have figured out they decide to watch over her.  The leader of the group, Doug (Ben Affleck) takes on the task, and eventually meets her, gets to know her, and...well, you might be able to guess where it's going, as this helps to cause complications with the rest of the group, especially his childhood friend (Jeremy Renner) and as Doug starts to question how much longer he wants to maintain his life of crime.<br /><br />Ben Affleck's second film as director is a solid genre piece, with good-to-excellent performances for the most part, but there's no question that it's in some ways too beholden to typical crime genre tropes, and rather predictable.  We have Doug, the kid from the old neighborhood (Charlestown, an old neighborhood of Boston that used to be an independent city) who unthinkingly follows a life of crime (bank and armored car robberies) until he realizes there might be a reason to stop; we've got his best buddy (Renner) who keeps guilting him into staying at it, and who is more than a borderline psycho; and the incredibly cold-blooded mob leader who runs the whole operation and won't let anybody leave it (Pete Postlethwaite); the tough FBI agent who has to be almost as dirty as the criminals (Jon Hamm); the victim-girlfriend who doesn't know about the life of crime (Rebecca Hall) and who represents a better life - being educated and not from the projects and all; the scorned, sleazy ex-girlfriend (Blake Lively), and a plot that mostly goes from a to b to c.<br /><br />But Affleck has a decent visual sense, a really good eye for the neighborhood details, and a solid way of putting together an action sequence - the robberies and car chases are exciting and punchy without relying too much on the typical shaky-cam that to my mind has ruined a lot of action cinema over the past decade.  And he's got an excellent touch with his actors, particularly the women - I would be astonished and disappointed if the British Hall, disappearing completely into an American character, doesn't get some awards attention, despite some thinness in the writing of her character.  I'm not sure that I'm crazy about Affleck's own performance, but his character more than most is hurt by the generic qualities of the script, and the relationship with Hall never comes off as terribly believable from either character's POV.  And there's an attempt I think that grows over the course of the running time at making this story out to be something more than an action-thriller (especially in the rather pretentious snippet of closing narration) that doesn't entirely work - the film can't decide ultimately between being a drama and an action film and isn't capable of handling both smoothly at the same time.  I think we'd have to be more sympathetic to Affleck's character to really get the whole \\"tragedy of a life in the projects\\" sense that is building; but I for one can't really see a guy who is still willing to fire automatic weapons at cops, with bystanders in close proximity, even after he's claimed that he's going to change his life, as anything like a hero.<br /><br />Still, despite some fairly significant problems in the screenplay and characterizations, this adds up to a very entertaining piece of work, and Affleck's sense of Boston and the urban terrain is exciting and very solidly real.  I hope he continues to make pictures in the city where he grew up; New York and L.A. get enough visibility in films.  Those who haven't seen a zillion other films of this type and who respond a little bitbetter to the central romantic/dramatic arc of the story will probably love this film, and I'll certainly be catching up with GONE BABY GONE, Affleck's first film as director, and with Rebecca Hall's earlier performances.ramatic arc of the story will probably love this film, and I'll certainly be catching up with GONE BABY GONE, Affleck's first film as director, and with Rebecca Hall's earlier performances.

1439156816|5.0|I have never read anything by Stephen King since I don't read his genre.  However, when this book was suggested as a valuable reference at a writer's seminar, I decided to pick it up.  What a delight! The ideas I had in my head about this author went right out the window.  The first third, which is a C.V., I found very refreshing.  Here is a man who is willing to tell the whole world about his successes and failures, his achievements and his weaknesses.  I found him humble and truthful, not talking down to the reader but putting himself on my level.  I got the feeling that he still has to pinch himself sometimes to realize his great success as a a popular fiction writer. I was very taken with his dedication as a family man.<br /><br />The second part is Toolbox which reveals his recommended tools of writing. Vocabular should be the first tool and do not make a conscious effort to improve it. Attempting to dress up your vocabulary will not produce a good product - he recommends to use the first word that comes to your mind, if it is appropriate and colorful.  Next is grammar which includes the basic parts of speech. Nouns, the words that name,  and verbs, the words that act, are the indispensable parts of writing.  In this section he describes his dislikes which include adverbs, the use of the passive voice and dialogue attribution.  These three rules are so golden that I have started back through my own writing to make sure these errors have not crept in.  The third layer in the toolbox is elements of style and King refers frequently to the Bible on this subject, The Elements of Style by Strunk and White. King puts forth the idea that the paragraph is the basic unit of writing. The quicken in the pace of a story comes at the paragraph level.<br /><br />The third part is On Writing. He groups writers into a pyramid, at the bottom the bad ones, next the competent writers, good writers and great writers.  He gives examples of each type and talks about the weaknesses of himself and others writers.  He says that good writing consists of mastering the fundamentals and then making a good writer out of a competent writer by hard work, dedication and timely help.  He claims it is impossible to make a competent writer out of a bad one.  The talent to write comes as a natural born creativity that can be honed.  Great writers are not made, they are born great. In order to be a writer, one must read a lot and write a lot.  I agree.  King recommends to write what you like and then make it unique with your own personal knowledge of life. I think that is one of the reasons why he says a writer must read a lot.  It helps to build the necessary knowledge.  King admits his style of writing is to create a situation and let the characters deal with it rather than have a set plot or story to begin with.  It works for him, maybe not for others.  Descriptions should include only what is necessary to  make the reader a sensory participant.  Dialogue must be true.  Well-crafted dialogue tells a lot about the character and can be used to reveal facts. Once a story is on paper the writer must think about what it means and enrich the following drafts with that conclusion.<br /><br />He suggests writing a whole first draft with the office door closed,  distractions kept to a minimum and no outside critique.  Write as fast a you can exactly as things come to your mind. Then let the whole thing set for a period of time, weeks. Then  reread the whole thing over making notes, fixing misspellings, picking up inconsistencies, looking for glaring holes in plot or character.  Finally, open the door of the office and let other trusted friends and colleagues see it.  The Ideal Reader will help you gauge whether the story is paced right and if the back story is satisfactory.  Back story is the stuff that happened before the story began. It helps define character and establish motivation. A second draft should be 10% shorter than the original. King also talks about writing courses and seminars, the benefit of these being that the desire to writer is taken seriously. He ends this section with a brief description of how a writer may proceed to get published.  Be your own advocate, do not forget magazines as a first publishing venue, and you should be able to get an agent if your work is salable or has promise.<br /><br />The last section of the book, On Living: A Postscript, describes his nearly fatal auto-pedestrian accident that stopped this book in its writing.  I was very saddened by the suffering caused by his serious injuries, but he went back to work only a month later.  This description helped me understand that life has very serious interruptions and to continue to write after a horrible experience can help a person recover and heal.  Life will bring us all trials and as writers, our love of writing is a solid rock to cling to along with our faith, our family and our friends.<br /><br />I would recommend this book to any fledgling writer for the view it gives of the heart of a writer and his ideas of what works.  I read  a copy from the library but plan to purchase a one for my reference library.seminars, the benefit of these being that the desire to writer is taken seriously. He ends this section with a brief description of how a writer may proceed to get published.  Be your own advocate, do not forget magazines as a first publishing venue, and you should be able to get an agent if your work is salable or has promise.      The last section of the book, On Living: A Postscript, describes his nearly fatal auto-pedestrian accident that stopped this book in its writing.  I was very saddened by the suffering caused by his serious injuries, but he went back to work only a month later. Life will bring us all trials and as writers, our love of writing is a solid rock to cling to along with our faith, our family and our friends.      I would recommend this book to any fledgling writer for the view it gives of the heart of a writer and his ideas of what works.
