0765326353|3.0|First off I listened to this book on audio tape (unabridged).  I did not read the physical copy of the book, so there may have been extra stuff in the book like maps or pictures, that I didn't see.<br /><br />I broke down below, what is good, bad and ugly about this book.  But to sum up, if you don't want to read my book long review, don't read this book for good dialogue, clever insight, philosophy, or multi-dimensional characters.  Read this book for the battle sequences, the cool new world the author has created, and the intrigue and mystery you will find in its pages.<br /><br />THE GOOD<br /><br />Mr. Sanderson does a great job at building a new world, with exciting new possibilities.  He goes into a lot of detail, from currency (an important detail) to plants, to city designs, to values in different socieites.  When you are reading, you get the impression that the history books referenced in the Way of Kings actually exist.  And the fact that he starts off many chapters with quotes from books, helps more completely draw you into this new world.<br /><br />Sanderson also does a great job of building a lot of mystery and intrigue.  There are a lot of mythical heroes/villains from the past (the heralds, dawn singers, radiants, voidbringers, etc.) to keep you curious.  I think its clever, that Sanderson creates only a few characters who actually believe in these mythical heroes/villains, while most educated people see them merely as myths.  This helps build the suspense and anticipation in the reader - are they real or not?  what are they like if they are real?  what powers do they have?  why did they dissappear? - that helps keep you interested.<br /><br />The last 200 or pages or so is exciting.  Sanderson does a great job of building the suspense and delivering a good emotional payoff - the flashback to Kaledon (sp?) watching his brother die, is brilliantly placed by Sanderson, right before Kaledon finally explodes into action.<br /><br />THE BAD<br /><br />The story of Shallon is very tiresome.  She thinks she's clever, but she comes across as an annoying know-it-all teenager, who really isn't as clever or smart, as the author thinks she is.  At times I remember saying to myself, please, please just kill her off.<br /><br />Kaledon's story is by the far the most interesting in the book, but Sanderson bogs it down in flashbacks.  Some of these flashbacks are helpful, and necessary, but most are annoying and hard to get through.  Furthermore, Kaledon's story goes on life support in the middle of the book.  Sanderson seems to have wanted to torture his readers and make them feel like they were on the bridge crew too.  I kept telling myself, escape already!!  I can't take anymore of your whining about everyone dying around you!  I almost didn't make it past this part.<br /><br />Dalinar's story is also really drawn out and annoying, until the end.  Much like Kaledon, Dalinar also spends most of the middle of the book being uninteresting and whiny.  Only in the end does he start to get interesting.<br /><br />THE UGLY<br /><br />The dialogue is painful most of the time.  Sanderson has no talent for crafting clever dialogue, and always seems to put in extra dialogue to explain things that are already obvious to the reader.<br /><br />The plot devices are transparent, and help disrupt some of the good things Sanderson does.<br /><br />There are logical inconsistencies, which Sanderson recognizes, and tries to resolve, unsuccessfully.<br /><br />The philosophical exchanges are painful to listen to.<br /><br />Many of the characters are flat and uninteresting.ever, but she comes across as an annoying know-it-all teenager, who really isn't as clever or smart, as the author thinks she is. Some of these flashbacks are helpful, and necessary, but most are annoying and hard to get through.  Furthermore, Kaledon's story goes on life support in the middle of the book.  Sanderson seems to have wanted to torture his readers and make them feel like they were on the bridge crew too.  I kept telling myself, escape already!!  I can't take anymore of your whining about everyone dying around you!  I almost didn't make it past this part.    Dalinar's story is also really drawn out and annoying, until the end.  Much like Kaledon, Dalinar also spends most of the middle of the book being uninteresting and whiny.  Only in the end does he start to get interesting.    THE UGLY    The dialogue is painful most of the time.  Sanderson has no talent for crafting clever dialogue, and always seems to put in extra dialogue to explain things that are already obvious to the reader.    The plot devices are transparent, and help disrupt some of the good things Sanderson does.    There are logical inconsistencies, which Sanderson recognizes, and tries to resolve, unsuccessfully.    The philosophical exchanges are painful to listen to.    Many of the characters are flat and uninteresting.

1439152780|5.0|Okay, that infamous line is never used, but it might as well have been.  There WERE plenty of dark and stormy nights in this deliciously atmospheric novel of suspense.  Like Ms. Morton's previous novels, this is a tale told in two times.  The \\"contemporary\\" story is set in 1992, and events are set in motion by the delivery of a letter 51 years late.  Protagonist Edie Burchill is visiting her parents when the letter arrives, and she witnesses her mother's unexpected and unexplained emotional response to the missive.<br /><br />Questioning her mother, Meredith, Edie learns for the first time that her mother was evacuated from London during WWII.  For over a year, she lived in the country with the sisters Blythe and their elderly father at gothic Castle Middlehurst.  Meredith is inexplicably reticent to discuss her past.  This is merely one more example of the distance that Edie has always felt with her mother.  Edie finds the incident odd, but it fades quickly into the past--until months later, lost on a road trip, she stumbles upon Castle Middlehurst and her curiosity is fiercely awakened.  On a whim, Edie arranges a tour of the castle and discovers, among other things, that all three sisters are alive and in residence.  After several introductory chapters setting up the story, the book moves back and forth between Edie's answer-seeking in 1992, and chapters set during the actual events that occurred between 1939 and 1941, seen from the POV of several of the story's participants.<br /><br />There is SO much more to the story told in this epic novel.  The Blythes are a literary family, and patriarch Raymond is the author of the children's classic The True History of the Mud Man that inspired Edie's love of literature and eventual career in publishing.  Ms. Morton is a brilliant story-teller and knows exactly how to torture her readers with questions.  What was in the letter Meredith received half a century late?  What was the true inspiration of the Mud Man?  Why is the parlor door kept locked?  What was in Raymond's will?  What really happened that night in 1941?<br /><br />So many questions.  And Morton teases us along for hundreds of pages, stringing along answers like breadcrumbs for readers to follow.  Kate Morton is very, very good at what she does.  Though, after three novels, the similarities in the types of stories she tells and the themes therein have become quite evident.  She's going to need to shake things up before she starts to recycle too much.  But for now, The Distant Hours is hard to beat for good old-fashioned entertainment value.  It literally brought chills and goose bumps to my skin time and time again.  Savor it on a dark and stormy night!kept locked?  What was in Raymond's will?  What really happened that night in 1941? 0823095223|5.0|I spent about ten years of my life writing and helping to promote popular collectibles from action figures to resin busts.  It's very easy for an outsider to be dismissive of the art of such things - who didn't own a Princess Leia action figure that made her look more simian than human.  But the truth is, the mass manufacturing process means that sometimes a lot of detail gets lost.  Certainly in my time I saw the standards of sculpting rise from the barely acceptable to the truly stunning.  I dare anyone to walk into the Four Horsemen's studios and look at the Masters of the Universe 2-Ups (basically a prototype at twice the finished size) and not have their breath taken away.    Tim Bruckner is one, if not the best, in the business.  He's a pro's pro - a sculptor other sculptors look up to.  If you've ever seen DC Direct's Alex Ross-inspired Justice Line you can see a lot of his best work.  In fact he's worked on a lot of DC Direct's properties bringing various comic artists' drawings into the 3D realm.    So now Tim has teamed up with Zach Oat of Toyfare fame and Rubn Procopio (Electric Tiki amongst other things) to bring us the definitive book on creating action figures and statues.    You'd think with the number of customizers and people wanting to break into the industry there would be a wealth of books on the subject, but this is the first one I've ever seen, and frankly, after being amazed by how comprehensive it is, I don't think another one is ever going to be needed.    What's stunning about this book is the level of detail.  It goes into everything, filled not just with information, but tips as well.  It starts with looking at the art reference, goes through the sculpting and casting to painting and ends with tips on going pro.  Gorgeous photography detailing the various processes sit alongside little cartoon asides that not only raise a smile but raise pertinent points.    If you've ever marvelled at the level of art in an action figure or bust, thenthis is a fascinating book even if you have no intention of ever sculpting yourself.  You can just dip into it and always come back with some new piece of knowledge you never had before.  But if you're into customizing and hoping one day to turn it into a full-time job then this book is a god-send.  Seriously, in ten years' time I reckon this will be THE textbook every sculptor learns from.    As someone who worked on the fringes of the industry for ten years, it's an incredible book.  For anyone looking to get into the industry I'd say it's pretty much damn well essential.

0811870413|1.0|Tartine Bread- A Dissenting Viewpoint<br /><br />I have had my copy of Chad Robertson's \\"Tartine Bread\\" book for some time now and have read the posts here from those who have baked his breads. As has been pointed out by other posters, 100 pages are dedicated to recipes that use bread in them which is nice but isn't normally part of a \\"Bread Book\\". It is a beautiful book and the images take up many of the pages. The book was delayed in publishing for what seemed like forever. Several critics gave it rave reviews so I was hopeful.<br /><br />The text is written like it is aimed primarily at new bakers or those who have heard about the bakery in California and want to learn to bake the Tartine breads. The author talks about how anyone can pick up this book and make good bread using just the chapter on the Basic Country Loaf. Robertson details his basic formula and attempts to de-mystify bakers math so you learn to \\"think like a baker\\". The working formula for building the leavan calls for double the amount needed in the dough build. Then his representation of the recipe or formula is in my opinion very non standard and confusing. Additionally the discussion of bakers percents and the listed percents do not add up correctly with the amounts in the formula. The total hydration of his basic country bread is off by the amount of the leavan. The total amount of flour is also off by the amount of the flour in the leavan. I wonder if he really understands bakers percent math. Small mistakes are one thing but any professional baker would be or should be embarrassed by this interpretation of bakers percent math. A new baker will gain nothing useful by the confusion created on pages 47) para 1. and page 48. In addition the percent for salt is shown to be 2%, which is a common ratio for that ingredient but then since the flour in the leavan isn't included in the total flour, the amount shown in under the salt column is off by 10%. That mistake won't ruin the bread but, the instructor/author should stay true to the universally accepted use of Bakers Math.<br /><br />The concept of baking in a cast iron combo cooker is in my opinion, an accident waiting to happen. While covered baking has been demonstrated to be an effective way to avoid the venting issues in a gas oven, the weight of a dutch oven at scorching 450F heat being held upside down is very difficult to manage. Yes, it can be done but the results are attainable using any one of many far safer methods. Remember the book is aimed at folks who have never baked bread. Even the famed \\"No Knead\\" breads only have you removing a lid on a dutch oven.<br /><br />The one redeeming component of the book is the discussion of managing fermentation to manipulate the outcome. The idea of using the leavan prior to it's maximum activity is interesting. I made the basic country bread yesterday and found it to be demanding. The fermentation time for my room temperature was 5 hours, during which I had to stretch and fold every 30 minutes to build the gluten strength. The proof time ended up being 4 hours before I finally called an end to the wait and baked it in 2 sessions under a clay le choche bell cooker. I left the second loaf in  the oven unmolested for the full 20 minutes after removing the cover and got a darker color. The first loaf, I checked a few times and rotated the loaf which looses a lot of energy.<br /><br />I like the bread. A good crust and a moist crumb with mild sour flavor the first night. It's hard to say for sure but I'm not sure the starter feeding schedule lowered the sour notes. It's about the same as usual for my regular Pain au Leavan. I might do some further experiments with controlling fermentation and using the starter before it is at its maximum ripeness. This is the only area of the book where the method put forth is new and unique to me. This is an advanced technique in my opinion. It's difficult to determine how far along the activity has progressed by using your sense of smell. Waiting for it to begin to fall is at least a stage most people can understand, and knowing your starter will double or triple in a given length of time is quantifiable.<br /><br />I don't want to be taken as a mean spirit here. But. I feel  a responsibility to speak the truth where it conflicts with normal conventions. I appreciate that it is hard to get a book published and off to market. In my opinion this book is not for a new baker and maybe not for anyone who is just learning to bake with sourdough. You have to be experienced enough to know on your own that the formulas are all wrong. At the least you have to know that every other bread author in the world uses bakers math in a different way to arrive at a final dough. The in depth description of all of the phases of baking are well written and helpful but the basic concept of using bakers math is flawed. There are many good books on the market to help aspiring bakers learn the basics. Reinhart's \\"Bread Bakers Apprentice\\" is one, and Hamelman's \\"Bread\\" is another to name the first that come to mind. Daniel DeMuzio's \\"Bread Baking\\" or Dan Lepard's offerings are some others who are well known and follow standard conventions. In conclusion, my advice would be to pass this book over. Its basis is factually sloppy and the method is unnecessarily difficult.I don't mean to be harsh but, it is what it is. Lots of good books out there to choose from.

B002XTXFU8|3.0|Most people who know me know that Charles Bronson is pretty much my hero. Based on everything incredible the guy's done single-handedly: punching through bulletproof glass, doing hundreds of thousands of dollars worth of damage to jail roofs, evacuating an entire prison when he took a teacher hostage, and his many records on push ups and sit ups...the guy is a legend. I've read a few of his books, and follow his Solitary Fitness program as much as I can. It took me a while before I watched the movie, since the trailer I saw wasn't exactly captivating. But after reading many reviews about how 'captivating' and 'amazing' this movie is, I finally gave it a shot. Let me tell you that if you watch this movie without any knowledge of Bronson himself, you're not going to get much out of it. And if you've read his books about prison life and how he's managed to get himself thrown back in time after time (in his book, Bronson), you'll probably wonder why the director and writer chose to skip over all the interesting parts of his life and decide to make a tribute to Stanley Kubrick instead.    Because we all know the poor guy's still in prison, and that's where most of the movie takes place, it's hard to give a plot summary, especially given that there's no real plot. We start out with a brief background of Bronson's child life, showing that he wasn't abused or neglected by his parents or anything. He was just a ruffian from the get-go. Later on he robs a shop for very little money (I'm talkin' a few bucks here...well, pounds since he's in England) and ends up in jail for a few years. While in prison, Bronson makes a name for himself. Constantly fighting guards, beating up other prisoners, taking hostages, etc. He's moved from prison to prison and eventually to an asylum where he's drugged up, then moved to another prison and set free. But once he's out, Bronson robs another store, and goes back to prison again, starting the cycle all over again.    See, it sounds like it should be interesting. And I wasn't going into this movie expecting an action/comedy or anything, though when I was done watching it, I think that might've been a better genre choice than what they went with. The movie misses everything, and ends up being more of an art house kinda deal more than anything. It's for the kind of person who watches A Clockwork Orange repeatedly, or tries to make sense of the stupidly little details that will never be explained in The Shining (omg why are there two coffee cups on the hotel manager's table before Jack gets his own?!?!). In the movie, it's hard to care much for Bronson since most of the time he's moping around or going crazy beating people up. Here and there, the movie cuts to scenes of him talking to an audience while wearing face paint and a nice suit, giving a brief narrative of what's going on or his thoughts. The problem is, after reading the book Bronson, this doesn't seem like it's the right guy. In the movie, he goes off about how he loves prison, but in the book, he constantly goes on about his miserable he was and how he desperately wanted to do things that we take for granted, like walking on grass or running on the beach. Is there anything like that here so we can identify with him, or make him seem more like a human? Nope. Even the part where he's drugged up in the asylum, which was a VERY big low point in the man's life, is over with in no time, and the scene's handled in such a bizarre way that I don't get why they even bothered to put it in here.    Another thing that bugged me is how they didn't show anything regarding Bronson's superhuman feats. There are a few brief seconds of him doing push ups early on, but no mention of how many he can do. I know it seems petty, but read up on the guy's fitness regime and you'll see why it would've been nice to see something more than him punching guards for 5 seconds, talking to a dream-audience, or an awkward scene of Bronson visiting his uncle in a room full of womenand men dressed as women, as he sits on a couch with no one else really saying anything, having a fancy drink. See? It's -art-! There's no explanation but since it's art, it must have some deep message that only the highly intellectual person will understand, right?! Had they tried to make this more of a biography movie, I would've handled it better. But as it stands, the only good things I can say about it are that Tom Hardy does resemble Charles a bit (though I don't get why he didn't dye his mustache black) and he got into very good shape for the part. That and some of the music is good. Otherwise, there's nothing I liked about this movie at all, and it hurts to say that about something based on the legend. I was never that interested in the movie, and wished it would end within the first 20 minutes. Stick with the books. At least there the scene where he fights a dog isn't cut off as soon as the dog's unleashed- there he straight up tells you about how he crammed his arm down the dog's mouth and ripped out part of its lung.    The picture quality of the blu-ray is average. The movie's shot in a gritty style to match the content, so don't expect this to look as pristine as some of the newer movies where everything looks flawless. I actually saw some white spots on the print in some scenes too. The audio's a bit better, and makes great use of the DTS track, though this isn't an action movie that could use it to the full capability. The music and ambiance really sets the mood in some scenes that are otherwise dull. Whenever Bronson paces the cell in his chains, you really feel like he's circling around you, with the clink-clink-clink going around.    The extras are surprisingly good, starting with an audio recording from the real Bronson himself. It plays for almost 20 minutes, and the kicker is that prison guards didn't know how it got recorded since visitors are heavily searched and remove nearly everything upon talking to him. What's funny is that if you listento this right after watching the movie, you'll see that Tom Hardy doesn't really sound like Bronson that much. Then there's 48 minutes of interviews with the director, writer and stars that cover nearly everything you could want to know about the making of the movie, almost like a commentary track. There's a standard making-of feature, the trailer and some b-roll footage, nothing special. But my favorite feature aside from the audio recording is the short Training Tom Hardy extra. It goes into detail about how he trained for 5 weeks to get into as good of shape as The Bronco.    All in all, the blu-ray might be fairly good, but the movie isn't. I'm sure I'll get comments with people telling me that I didn't 'get' the movie, but I really don't care. It was uninteresting and didn't capture the essence of the man. A gentle giant on one side, zero tolerance for being pushed around on the other. Again, I highly recommend at least reading his Solitary Fitness book to get a better idea of how strong the guy is, and reading the book Bronson to see that the guy isn't dull like he is here.
